Q: did you have any training in connection with your worked on the RMS And the other o opioids product you mentioned in terms of -- in terms of the Controlled Substances Act?
A: Yes, absolutely. Training on the Controlled Substances Act was mandatory when you were working with Schedule II products like OxyContin. Purdue had pretty comprehensive training programs that covered all the DEA requirements and regulations that applied to our work.

We had to understand the basics of the CSA framework - the different controlled substance schedules, what Schedule II meant in terms of prescribing limitations, storage and security requirements, record-keeping obligations. There was training on proper documentation procedures, understanding prescription monitoring, recognizing signs of potential diversion or abuse.

I remember sessions on working appropriately with healthcare providers - understanding their DEA registration requirements, proper prescribing protocols, patient monitoring responsibilities. We also covered the sales rep's role in all of this - what we could and couldn't do, documentation we needed to maintain, reporting obligations if we observed anything suspicious.

There were refresher trainings on a regular basis too, not just initial onboarding. The regulatory environment was always evolving, and Purdue wanted to make sure their sales force stayed current on compliance requirements. Sometimes there would be updates when DEA issued new guidance or when company policies were revised.

It was pretty thorough training, actually. You couldn't be effective selling controlled substances without understanding the regulatory framework you were operating within. Both from a compliance standpoint and from being able to have credible conversations with physicians about proper use and monitoring of these medications.

[Turn 23]
Q: What do you mean oy that?
A: Well, when I said you couldn't be effective without understanding the regulatory framework, I meant that physicians and other healthcare providers expect pharmaceutical reps to be knowledgeable about the compliance requirements that affect their prescribing practices.

If a doctor has questions about DEA registration requirements, or proper storage procedures, or documentation they need to maintain when prescribing Schedule II substances, they expect the sales rep to be able to provide accurate information or at least know where to direct them for answers. If you don't understand those requirements yourself, you lose credibility pretty quickly.

Also, part of being effective in that space means understanding the legitimate concerns healthcare providers have about controlled substances - the additional regulatory burden, the scrutiny from regulatory agencies, the liability issues they face. You need to be able to address those concerns knowledgeably rather than just pushing product features.

And from a compliance standpoint, the company needs its sales force to recognize potential red flags - unusual prescribing patterns, inappropriate use requests, anything that might suggest diversion or abuse. You can't identify those issues if you don't understand what normal, legitimate use looks like within the regulatory framework.

So it's really about being a knowledgeable resource for healthcare providers while also fulfilling your compliance obligations to the company and to regulatory authorities. You can't do either of those things effectively without solid training on the Controlled Substances Act requirements.

I also interfaced regularly with our compliance and legal teams, especially on the controlled substances products, to ensure our marketing and sales activities were appropriate under CSA requirements and company policies.

[Turn 120]
Q: What -- what is a oulk tote exactly?
A: A bulk tote is a large container used for shipping and storing pharmaceutical products in bulk quantities. It's basically a big box or container that holds a large amount of product - much larger than individual bottles that go to retail pharmacies.

Pharmacies, especially larger ones or wholesalers, might order products in bulk totes because it's more cost-effective for high-volume products. Instead of getting hundreds of individual bottles, they get one large container they can then break down and repackage as needed.

This would be particularly relevant for high-volume generic products like hydrocodone where a pharmacy might go through large quantities. The bulk tote gives them a more efficient way to receive and manage inventory.

But again, if you're asking about bulk totes specifically mentioned in that 2004 document you were reading from, I wouldn't have been involved in those specific marketing materials since I wasn't at Mallinckrodt then.

[Turn 130]
Q: And what was NACDS?
A: NACDS is the National Association of Chain Drug Stores. It's a major trade association that represents chain pharmacies and drug stores across the country.

They hold an annual trade show and conference that's one of the biggest events in the retail pharmacy industry. All the major pharmacy chains attend - CVS, Walgreens, Rite Aid, regional chains, independent pharmacies, wholesalers like McKesson, Cardinal Health, AmerisourceBergen.

For pharmaceutical companies like Mallinckrodt, NACDS is an important show because that's where you meet with your key retail pharmacy customers, showcase your products, discuss business opportunities, negotiate contracts sometimes.

It's basically where the retail pharmacy industry gathers once a year. Very important for generic drug companies especially because the chain pharmacies and wholesalers are such big customers for generic products.

During my time at Mallinckrodt, NACDS would have been one of our most important trade shows to attend because so many of our key customers for our generic opioid products would be there - the chains, the wholesalers, the buying groups.

[Turn 167]
Q: So your understanding of chargeback data, as you sit here right now, is that you would not be a.ole to see any of your downstream customers, so you would only be able to see sales to your direct customers, but you had no access to any cata for any of their customers. Is that your unc. erstanc ing?
A: No, that's not correct. Chargeback data actually does give you visibility into downstream sales beyond just your direct customers.

When a wholesaler sells to a pharmacy or other end customer at a contracted price that's lower than what they paid us, they submit a chargeback to recover that difference. So the chargeback data shows us not just sales to our direct customers like the wholesalers, but also provides visibility into where those products are ultimately going and at what volumes.

The chargeback information includes details about the end customers - the pharmacies, hospitals, or other entities that the wholesaler sold to. So we can see downstream sales patterns, not just our direct sales to wholesalers.

This gives pharmaceutical companies much more granular visibility into the market than just tracking direct customer sales. You can see which pharmacy chains are dispensing your products, geographic distribution patterns, volume trends at different types of customers.

So chargeback data is actually a key source of market intelligence that goes well beyond just direct customer sales. It helps you understand the full distribution chain and end-market performance.

A: Yes, that's correct. When AmerisourceBergen or other wholesalers submit chargeback data to us, we can see the end customer information - whether it was sold to a pharmacy, a pain clinic, a hospital, or other types of customers.

The chargeback contains the details of that downstream transaction, including who the end customer was and what type of entity they are. So we'd have visibility into whether our hydrocodone products were going to retail pharmacies, hospital pharmacies, pain management clinics, or other types of dispensing locations.

This level of detail in the chargeback data is important for understanding distribution patterns and market trends. You can see which types of customers are purchasing your products and in what volumes, even though you're not selling directly to them.

So yes, the chargeback system provides that downstream visibility beyond just our direct sales to the wholesaler.

[Turn 305]
Q: Do you know what methadone is used for?
A: Yes, methadone is primarily used for opioid addiction treatment. It's a long-acting opioid medication that's used in methadone maintenance therapy programs to help people who are addicted to other opioids like heroin or prescription painkillers.

The way it works is that methadone can prevent withdrawal symptoms and reduce cravings without producing the same euphoric high as other opioids when taken as prescribed. It allows people with opioid addiction to stabilize their lives and function normally while they work on recovery.

Methadone is also sometimes used for chronic pain management, but its primary and most well-known use is in opioid addiction treatment programs. It's a heavily regulated controlled substance that's typically dispensed through specialized methadone clinics or treatment facilities.

So if methadone is appearing on this Mallinckrodt generic products list from 2007, that would indicate we were manufacturing generic methadone products, likely for use in addiction treatment programs.

These would be programs designed to help ensure that the benefits of a drug continue to outweigh the risks, and they can include things like special training for healthcare providers, patient education materials, monitoring requirements, or restricted distribution systems.

They might include things like pricing information, promotional messaging, product identification details, or other marketing materials that would be visible to wholesalers when they're handling the products in their warehouses or distribution centers.

The idea would be to provide additional marketing or promotional messaging at the wholesale level - so when wholesalers like McKesson, Cardinal Health, or AmerisourceBergen are handling the products, they would see these stickers with whatever promotional messaging or information we wanted to communicate.

But I can't recall the specific details of what particular wholesaler stickers Mallinckrodt was using or what exactly was on them during my time there. It was one of various marketing tools that could be used, but I don't have clear recollection of the specifics of how we implemented that particular approach.

[Turn 486]
Q: And how would that pe a marketing tool?
A: Well, wholesaler stickers would serve as a marketing tool by keeping your product and promotional messaging visible at the wholesale level.

When wholesalers are handling products in their warehouses - picking, packing, shipping - those stickers would be a constant reminder of whatever promotional offer or messaging you're trying to communicate. It could be pricing information, volume discounts, rebate offers, or other incentives.

The idea is that when wholesale staff are physically handling your products, they see that promotional messaging repeatedly. That could influence their recommendations to customers, or make them more aware of special offers that they might then communicate to their retail customers.

It's also a way to differentiate your product on the shelf in the wholesale environment. If there are competing products sitting next to each other, having visible promotional stickers could draw attention to your specific product and any special offers or advantages you're promoting.

So it's really about maintaining visibility for your promotional messaging throughout the distribution chain, not just at the end customer level but also at the wholesale distribution level where decisions about product handling and customer recommendations might be influenced.

[Turn 504]
Q: What is API again?
A: API stands for Active Pharmaceutical Ingredient. That's the actual drug substance - the chemical compound that provides the therapeutic effect in a medication.

In pharmaceutical manufacturing, the API is the core ingredient that makes the medicine work, as opposed to the inactive ingredients like fillers, binders, coatings, and other excipients that are used to create the final dosage form like a tablet or capsule.

So when we talk about API in the pharmaceutical industry, we're referring to the actual drug substance itself - the active ingredient that produces the desired medical effect.

[Turn 505]

I'm not making a connection between API and something called a "Narcotics Story." If there was something at Mallinckrodt that was referred to as a "Narcotics Story," it wasn't terminology I used for API or that I specifically recall from my time there.

[Turn 508]
Q: Are you familiar with this law that was set for January 1st, 2009, in California?
A: I have a general familiarity with California's pedigree law requirements. California was implementing stricter track-and-trace requirements for pharmaceuticals, particularly for what they classified as dangerous drugs, which would include controlled substances like opioids.

The law was designed to create a better paper trail - or electronic trail - to track pharmaceutical products as they moved through the supply chain from manufacturer to wholesaler to pharmacy. The requirement for unique identifiers at the smallest packaging level was part of ensuring that each individual unit could be tracked and traced.

This was a significant compliance issue for pharmaceutical manufacturers and distributors because it required changes to packaging, labeling, and tracking systems. It makes sense that a major distributor like McKesson would have concerns about implementation and would want to work with manufacturers like us to ensure smooth compliance.

But I don't recall all the specific details of exactly how that law was structured or what all the requirements were. I remember it being a significant regulatory change that required coordination between manufacturers and distributors to implement properly.

The pharmaceutical industry was dealing with various concerns about product integrity, counterfeiting, and ensuring legitimate distribution channels. These tracking requirements were part of broader efforts to create better oversight of pharmaceutical distribution.

I knew that controlled substances and certain other medications required special handling and tracking due to their regulatory classification, but I understood the pedigree law requirements primarily in terms of the compliance and operational changes we needed to make - things like the unique identifiers, documentation requirements, and coordination with our distributors like McKesson to ensure smooth implementation.

[Turn 558]
Q: And do you know that there were sort of gracations of -- of c angers associatec. with these sorts of crugs?
A: Yes, I understood that there were gradations or different levels of danger and abuse potential associated with controlled substances. That's essentially what the scheduling system under the Controlled Substances Act is designed to reflect.

You have Schedule I substances which have the highest abuse potential with no accepted medical use, then Schedule II with high abuse potential but accepted medical uses, Schedule III with moderate abuse potential, Schedule IV with lower abuse potential, and Schedule V with the lowest abuse potential among controlled substances.

So yes, I was aware that the scheduling system was designed to reflect different gradations of danger, abuse potential, and risk of dependence. The higher the schedule number, generally the lower the abuse potential and associated dangers, with Schedule II being among the most dangerous in terms of abuse potential while still having legitimate medical uses.

That's why Schedule II products like our opioids had the most stringent regulatory controls - because they represented some of the highest levels of abuse potential and associated dangers among substances that still had accepted medical applications.

[Turn 559]
Q: Do you have an understanding of what a Schec ule I drug is?
A: Yes, Schedule I drugs are controlled substances that have a high potential for abuse and no currently accepted medical use in treatment in the United States. They're considered to have no accepted medical use and a lack of accepted safety for use under medical supervision.

Examples would be things like heroin, LSD, marijuana at the federal level - substances that the DEA has determined have high abuse potential but no accepted medical applications.

Schedule I substances are essentially prohibited from medical use and can only be used for very limited research purposes under strict DEA oversight. They represent the most restrictive classification under the Controlled Substances Act.

So while Schedule II substances like our opioid products have high abuse potential but accepted medical uses, Schedule I substances are deemed to have that same high abuse potential but without any accepted medical benefit, which is why they're essentially banned from medical practice.

[Turn 560]
Q: And so what was your and erstanding of what a Schedule II products was when you were there?
A: My understanding was that Schedule II products were controlled substances that had a high potential for abuse which may lead to severe psychological or physical dependence, but they also had accepted medical uses in treatment in the United States.

So these were the products that sat right below Schedule I in terms of abuse potential and danger, but unlike Schedule I substances, they had legitimate medical applications that justified allowing their use under strict regulatory controls.

The Schedule II classification meant these products required the most stringent controls for any substance that was still available for medical use - things like special manufacturing quotas, detailed record-keeping, secure storage requirements, special prescription requirements, and extensive DEA oversight and reporting.

So my understanding was that our Schedule II opioid products were among the most dangerous controlled substances that were still permitted for medical treatment. They had significant abuse potential and could cause severe dependence, but they also provided important pain relief benefits for patients when used appropriately under medical supervision.

That's why they required such strict regulatory oversight - to try to balance the legitimate medical benefits against the significant risks of abuse and dependence.

If someone's questioning whether one of your major customers might have suspicious ordering patterns, and that customer represents a significant portion of your sales volume, your immediate reaction might be defensive because you're thinking about your commission, your bonus, your ability to make your targets for the year.

So yes, there's definitely a financial incentive for salespeople to want to preserve those relationships and those sales volumes, and that can make it emotionally charged when questions come up about whether there might be compliance issues with those accounts. It takes discipline to step back and look at things objectively when your personal income is directly affected by the outcome of those discussions.

That's probably why I was commending Victor for handling it in a non-emotional way despite the dollars at stake - because it would be natural for someone in his position to get defensive about it.

[Turn 893]
Q: Do you know what a lifestyle crug is?
A: Yes, a lifestyle drug is typically a medication that's used to treat conditions that aren't necessarily life-threatening or serious medical conditions, but rather to improve quality of life or address lifestyle-related issues. These might include things like medications for erectile dysfunction, weight loss drugs, cosmetic treatments, or medications for conditions that are more about comfort or appearance rather than critical medical needs. In the context of pharmaceutical distribution, lifestyle drugs can sometimes be associated with cash-pay customers or non-traditional dispensing patterns since they're often not covered by insurance or may be sought for non-medical purposes.

[Turn 894]
Q: did you ever hear any opioid products being referrec to as lifestyle crugs?
A: I... well, that's not really the traditional definition of what we would consider lifestyle drugs. Opioids are controlled substances for pain management, which is a legitimate medical purpose. But I suppose if someone was using that term in the context of opioids, they might be referring to situations where opioids were being used for non-medical purposes or in ways that weren't for legitimate pain management. 

I can't recall specifically hearing opioid products being referred to as lifestyle drugs in my time at Mallinckrodt, but I understand the concern behind the question - if opioids were being dispensed more for recreational use or abuse rather than legitimate medical needs, someone might characterize that as lifestyle use rather than medical use. But that wouldn't be appropriate or legal use of opioid products.

[Turn 895]

C-III and C-IV products have progressively lower abuse potential and diversion risk compared to C-II.

So for Sunrise to say they're avoiding C-III and C-IV products due to diversion risk while continuing to distribute C-II products doesn't make sense from a legitimate compliance standpoint. It's completely backwards. If they were truly concerned about diversion risk, C-II products would be the first ones they'd want to avoid or be most careful with, not the last ones they'd continue distributing.

This statement in their narrative actually makes their business model look even more suspicious, because it suggests they understood diversion risks but were making decisions that don't align with legitimate pharmaceutical distribution practices.
